</details>

I now have a rich picture across your body of work, past IIM SG session, and current research. Let me synthesize this into a comprehensive plan.


---

## Four 90-Minute Workshops for IIM Alumni Singapore

### Audience read

From your December 2025 IIM Alumni SG session, this audience arrives with: FOMO but embarrassment about gaps, jargon overwhelm, sharp domain expertise they undervalue, and a strong "show me, don't tell me" orientation. They are fast learners who benchmark everything against ROI. Singapore context adds: 72% of Singapore businesses plan to deploy agentic AI in several operational areas within two years, up from 15% today, and data sovereignty is a live concern for 77%. That urgency is your tailwind. [Deloitte](https://www.deloitte.com/southeast-asia/en/about/press-room/agentic-and-physical-ai-set-for-rapid-growth-in-singapore-in-the-next-two-years.html)

Your Dec 2025 session already covered Vibe Analysis basics (WhatsApp, Google Takeout, "Analyze" prompts, investigative journalist mode). **Don't repeat it.** These four new workshops build on top.


---

### Workshop 1: Context Engineering

#### *"Why your domain knowledge is now worth more than the AI itself"*

**Core thesis:** The model is commoditised. What your AI *knows about you and your business* is the new competitive moat. When every company has access to the same foundation models, the differentiator is no longer which model you use — it's what your model knows about your business. The organizations pulling ahead aren't the ones with the biggest AI budgets; they're the ones that have turned their institutional knowledge into machine-readable context. [Atlan](https://atlan.com/know/what-is-context-engineering/)

**Why this matters to IIM alumni specifically:** Your BCG deck, your client network, your 20-year mental model of your industry — that's *context*. And most of you are leaving it on the table.


---

**Arc (90 min)**

**0–15: The prompt engineering obituary** Open with the provocative claim: most prompt tips you've read are pseudoscience. From your Goodbye MBA talk: *"The prompt engineering techniques that were actually true a year ago are no longer true. In some cases, they actually make LLMs perform marginally worse."* Show the before/after: "You are an expert analyst. This is very important for my career" → worse results on newer models. In July 2025, Gartner declared "context engineering is in, and prompt engineering is out," urging AI leaders to prioritize context-aware architectures over clever prompting. What replaced it? [Atlan](https://atlan.com/know/what-is-context-engineering/)

**15–35: What context actually is (the 5 layers)**

* **Memory**: your past preferences, decisions, style (AGENTS.md)
* **Knowledge**: your documents, emails, reports, domain expertise
* **Instructions**: your role, goals, constraints
* **Tools**: what the AI is allowed to do (search, code, email)
* **Examples**: how good looks for *you* specifically

Demo live: open Claude, paste a bare prompt → mediocre output. Then paste the same prompt with a 5-sentence context block (who you are, what you're trying to do, what your industry is, what good looks like, what to avoid). Night-and-day difference. MIT's 2025 State of AI in Business study found 95% of enterprise AI pilots delivered zero measurable ROI — the root cause was not the models, but the lack of context. [Atlan](https://atlan.com/know/what-is-context-engineering/)

**35–55: Your digital exhaust as context** The IIM alumni edge: 20 years of emails, meeting transcripts, LinkedIn activity, WhatsApp chats. Show your own workflow from the blog: *"Export your WhatsApp chats, journal entries, email logs, bank statements, and feed them to an LLM. Ask it to find patterns in your behaviour, identify blind spots, or summarise your year."*

Then the professional version: your call transcripts → AI extracts your advice patterns → you have a reusable context file that makes every future conversation smarter. Demo: paste a call summary, ask AI to extract your decision principles. This is what you did for IITM Institutional Advancement — giving the agent your blog, transcripts, and TIL as context, then asking it to generate ideas grounded in *your* thinking.

**55–70: AGENTS.md — your personal AI constitution** Introduce the AGENTS.md concept: a file you give every AI session that tells it who you are. From your ai-advice.md: *"Every serious AI project needs: AGENTS.md (folder-specific instructions the agent reads on startup), prompts.md (versioned prompts), a skills/ folder (encapsulated workflows)."*

Walk through building a personal AGENTS.md live: name, role, expertise, communication style, recurring tasks, what to skip, what to always include. This is the "one-time setup that makes every session 10x better."

**70–90: Group exercise**

**"Build your context file in 15 minutes"**

Each participant creates a 200-word context block:

1. Who you are (role, industry, company size)
2. Your top recurring AI task
3. What "excellent output" looks like for your domain
4. What AI should never do/assume

Post to shared doc. Then: paste all 100 into Claude and ask it to identify patterns — *"What do Singapore MBA professionals most commonly need from AI?"* Show results live. The meta-insight is that the group's collective context is itself valuable intelligence.

**Innovative twist:** Run a "Context Olympics" — same generic prompt, same task (e.g., "prepare me for a board meeting"). Without context: generic. With context block: uncannily specific. Best before/after wins (voted by participants via poll).


---

### Workshop 2: AI Tools & Workflows

#### *"From occasional user to 50-times-a-day reflex"*

**Core thesis:** The difference between people who find AI useful and those who don't isn't the model — it's usage volume. At 5 uses a day, AI is a novelty. At 50, it's infrastructure.

**Why this matters for IIM alumni:** You're smart learners. But you're reaching for AI only on the "big" problems. The real ROI is in the 47 small ones you don't bother with.


---

**Arc (90 min)**

**0–15: The volume argument** From your ai-advice.md: *"Use AI 50 times a day across tiny personal, work, learning, and creative tasks. High volume forces you to find use-cases you'd otherwise ignore, and builds the reflex of reaching for AI first."*

Quick exercise: ask participants how many times they used AI yesterday. Poll shows typical answer: 2–5. Then ask: "How many times did you feel stuck, curious, annoyed, or uncertain?" That's the gap.

**15–40: The four workflow patterns every IIM alum needs**

1. **Meta-prompting** — "I don't know how to ask this. What should I ask you?" From Deloitte talk: volunteer used this to turn a vague brief into a precise prompt. Works for any domain they don't own.
2. **Cross-validation** — From Goodbye MBA: run the same question through Claude and ChatGPT. Disagreement = flag for review. Agreement = higher confidence. Correlations are low enough that this materially reduces error rate. The management skill: "Have AI cross-check AI."
3. **Style transfer** — From IIM SG Dec 2025: paste your boring policy document, ask for Malcolm Gladwell version. Works for medical reports, board papers, legal contracts. Demo live: take a mundane MAS circular and rewrite it as a thriller opening.
4. **Voice mode as a power tool** — From LBSNAA and ai-advice.md: *"Use voice mode on mobile to talk to AI while walking or thinking. 'Ramble' at it — it can structure your thoughts."* Demo: walk through a problem out loud, let ChatGPT transcribe and structure. This turns commute time into productive thinking time — especially relevant for Singapore's MRT crowd.

**40–60: Tool stack for senior professionals**

Not "which AI is best" — that changes monthly. Instead: *what job, which tool.*

| Task | Best tool (May 2026) |
| --- | --- |
| Writing, editing, aesthetics | Claude Sonnet |
| Deep research, fact-heavy | ChatGPT with extended thinking |
| Google Workspace integration | Gemini |
| Web research + synthesis | Perplexity |
| Audio/podcast from document | NotebookLM |

From your SG Analytics session: *"Use Claude Sonnet for daily work. Reserve Opus/extended thinking for high-complexity bursts."* And the principle you gave to IIM SG Dec 2025: "Get a $20 paid account. It's the highest ROI investment you'll make."

Also: the security nuance IIM alumni need — consumer plans vs enterprise API. From ai-advice.md: *"Enterprise and API plans protect business data by default. For sensitive work, use enterprise/API."* This is the one thing Singapore compliance-aware professionals care about.

**60–75: Personal workflow audit**

Each participant maps their week:

* Top 3 recurring tasks (report drafting, meeting prep, client research)
* Current time spent
* AI-able? (yes/no/partial)

Then: pick one task and spend 10 minutes building a prompt template for it. Share to a WhatsApp group. AI analyzes what the group's most common time sinks are.

**75–90: Live workflow build**

Take one volunteer's recurring task. Build a full workflow together in the session: define inputs → write prompt → test → meta-prompt to improve → save as template. Show that 20 minutes of setup saves 2 hours a week.

**Innovative exercise for 100 online:**

**"Task Race"** — Everyone gets the same brief: *"Prepare a 5-point talking brief for a board meeting on AI risk governance in Singapore."* They have 10 minutes. Share outputs. AI judges: most insightful, most concise, most original. Then debrief what prompt strategies produced the best results. The competitive element works well with IIM alumni.


---

### Workshop 3: Agentic Analysis

#### *"From analyst to conductor"*

**Core thesis:** You've been using AI as a smart search engine. Agents are different — they read, reason, code, and loop, working for hours without intervention. Your job shifts from doing the analysis to *directing the analysis*.

This builds on the Dec 2025 Vibe Analysis session, which was "AI does one analysis." This is: "AI runs the whole research project."


---

**Arc (90 min)**

**0–15: What's changed since Vibe Analysis**

In Dec 2025, you showed: give AI a file, ask it to analyse. Now the capability gap has widened dramatically. From the Verifiable Autonomy talk: *"METR data shows agents can now work 12 hours continuously. A rough trend: the task horizon is growing exponentially."* Concretely: a research agent can now take a company name, search 40 sources, extract signals, cross-check them, and generate a structured brief — without human intervention. From SG Analytics: *"AI agents can compress traditional research timelines from four to six weeks down to four to six hours."*

The shift to explain: you used to be the analyst. Now you're the editor and accountable partner. Like moving from rowing to steering.

**15–40: Live agentic analysis demo**

Take a real Singapore business question. Good options for IIM audience:

* "Who are the top 5 PE funds active in Southeast Asia infrastructure in the last 12 months, and what signals suggest their next move?"
* "What is the current competitive landscape for [participant's industry]?"
* "Analyse the AI adoption readiness of [a company they all know]"

Run it live through Claude with extended thinking and web search. Show the chain: search → extract → synthesise → cite sources → flag uncertainty. Use your Verifiable Autonomy demo structure: facts → inferences → known unknowns.

Key moment: show it getting something wrong. Then show cross-validation: run same question through ChatGPT. They disagree on one claim. *That's your signal to verify.* This is the management insight: disagreement is information.

From the Deloitte session: *"You could say, 'I'll have this for you by the time you reach home.' Not same month. Not same week. Same day. Arguably same hour."*

**40–60: Verification architecture for business leaders**

This is what makes agents usable in enterprise — from your Verifiable Autonomy talk with Ankor:

* 72% of Singapore businesses are planning to deploy agentic AI — but most will fail without verification architecture. [Deloitte](https://www.deloitte.com/southeast-asia/en/about/press-room/agentic-and-physical-ai-set-for-rapid-growth-in-singapore-in-the-next-two-years.html)
* The triage model: Green (auto-approve), Yellow (spot-check), Red (human required). Build this into any agent workflow.
* "LLMs hallucinate, but code doesn't" — for anything numerical or logical, always ask it to write and run code. From ai-advice.md: *"For math, analysis, or logic: tell it to write and run deterministic code rather than reason in prose. Code is binary — it works or it fails."*
* The golden set: what you showed at Prudential — build 20–30 examples of what "right" looks like, and use a second LLM to judge new outputs against them.

Practical frame for IIM alumni: *"This is just your management instinct — redundancy, cross-checks, escalation paths. Same principles, new domain."*

**60–75: Build your first analysis agent brief**

Exercise: each participant defines a real research question they face (competitive intelligence, market sizing, regulatory landscape, client background). Then writes a 5-line brief for an agent:

* What's the question?
* What sources to use?
* What output format?
* What to verify?
* What confidence level is acceptable?

This "agent brief" is the new skill. It's more valuable than knowing how to prompt.

**75–90: Red team the agent**

Take one agent-generated analysis from the session. Participants have 10 minutes to fact-check 5 claims using their own knowledge or a second AI. Who finds the most errors? This builds the *verification reflex* — the critical skill in the agent era.

**Innovative exercise for 100 online:**

**"The Research Race"** — Submit one business question per team (groups of 5). Each team has 20 minutes and any AI tool. Then: share outputs. A second AI (Claude, given all outputs) judges which is most accurate, most surprising, most actionable. Debrief what workflow produced the best results. The IIM crowd will love the competition.

**Bonus for Singapore context:** Point to the ECI (Enterprise Compute Initiative) and IMDA AI Verify framework as local resources that lower risk in deploying these agents.


---

### Workshop 4: AI Strategy

#### *"Which of your assets are appreciating and which are burning"*

**Core thesis:** AI doesn't just change what you *do* — it changes what is *valuable*. The strategic question is not "how do we use AI" but "which of our assets compound in value and which ones decay?"

This is the workshop IIM alumni have been unconsciously waiting for — it connects AI to the frameworks they already trust: competitive advantage, asset valuation, portfolio strategy.


---

**Arc (90 min)**

**0–20: The asset inversion**

Open with the provocation: *"Name an asset your organization has built over the last 10 years that AI doesn't erode."*

Common answers: brand, relationships, proprietary data, regulatory approvals, client trust. Work through each:

| Asset | Depreciates with AI | Appreciates with AI |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Junior analyst hours | ✓ (commoditised by agents) |  |
| Domain expertise (map) |  | ✓ (becomes context) |
| Proprietary data |  | ✓ (feeds better agents) |
| Process documentation | ✓ (anyone can replicate) |  |
| Relationship trust |  | ✓ (harder to fake) |
| Accountability |  | ✓ (scarcest in agentic era) |

From your Goodbye MBA: *"Sustainable differentiation lies not in the artifact itself, but accountability for the outcome and the trusted relationship that allows you to navigate the nuance."* From your ai-advice.md: *"Skills: what grows — asking questions, choosing valuable problems, validation, accountability, people skills, taste."*

From your Deloitte session, the answer to the commoditisation question: *"The only true sustainable source of advantage may be regulation and lobby... plus anchoring premium pricing to proprietary data, and assumption of liability."* This is provocative for MBA-trained strategists — challenge them on it.

**20–40: Where Singapore enterprise AI is actually going**

Ground the strategy in local data. Autonomous AI agents are racing into the enterprise, with 72% of businesses in Singapore planning to deploy agentic AI in several operational areas within two years, up from 15% today. Sovereign AI is a key consideration, as 77% of businesses in Singapore say that data residency and in-country or in-region compute considerations are important to their strategic planning. [Deloitte](https://www.deloitte.com/southeast-asia/en/about/press-room/agentic-and-physical-ai-set-for-rapid-growth-in-singapore-in-the-next-two-years.html)

Implications for IIM alumni:

* **Financial services** (DBS, OCBC, Prudential types in the room): verification architecture is now a regulatory requirement, not a nice-to-have. Your Verifiable Autonomy framework is the pitch.
* **Consulting/professional services**: the commodity shift is real and fast. The Deloitte session example: a 5-minute analysis that replaces 5 weeks of BCG work. Where does your firm's moat actually sit?
* **Founders/operators**: "Buy foundations, build thin orchestration." From ai-advice.md: *"Don't train models — they're soon obsolete. Don't build heavy platforms — they're quickly superseded. Do build: skills libraries, prompt repositories, verification layers, data pipelines, and MCP connectors."*

**40–60: Building your AI strategy on one page**

Framework: the three horizons of AI strategy (adapted for non-tech executives)

**Horizon 1 (Now — workflow productivity):** Which processes can be 10x'd in the next 3 months? Use the agent brief format from Workshop 3. Quick wins: research, drafting, analysis, meeting prep.

**Horizon 2 (6–18 months — context moats):** What institutional knowledge should become machine-readable context? Your CRM, your deal memos, your risk assessments — what feeds a proprietary intelligence layer competitors can't replicate?

**Horizon 3 (2+ years — new business models):** From your SG Analytics session: *"Research teams can transition to outcome-based pricing where clients pay per report based on satisfaction rather than fixed FTE costs."* What does your business look like when AI can do the production work and you charge for judgment?

From ai-advice.md: *"Adding is easier than changing. Using AI to improve existing work has high inertia and risk. Creating a new workflow or output has less competition."*

**60–75: The adoption trap and how to avoid it**

From ai-advice.md, what actually drives adoption:

* *"Show leaders using AI (not just talking about it)"* — this room is the lever
* *"Find AI enthusiasts. Top-down mandates build frustration."*
* *"Measure actual adoption — unique days of use, token consumption, task diversity — not training completion rates."*
* *"Use games and challenges to teach AI."*
* *"The most effective AI operators aren't necessarily the most experienced — they're the most willing to delegate, verify, and learn fast."*

The Singapore-specific unlock: ECI funding means the financial risk of experimentation is partly socialised. This changes the calculus for cautious organisations.

**75–90: Personal AI strategy exercise**

Each participant does a 10-minute "asset audit":

1. List your top 3 professional assets (skills, relationships, domain knowledge, data, reputation)
2. AI question: does this depreciate (AI can replicate it) or appreciate (AI amplifies it)?
3. What's your highest-leverage AI investment in the next 90 days?

Share to shared doc. AI clusters into patterns by role/industry. You debrief live: *"Singapore IIM alumni collectively believe their most appreciating asset is [X] and their biggest AI threat is [Y]."* This is a live market research exercise that makes the room its own case study.

**Innovative closing:** The "impossibility list." Ask each participant to submit one task they believe AI genuinely cannot do well (yet). Collect all 100. Then — over the next month — you systematically test them and share results. This becomes a living community research project. Subscribers who submitted get the results. It's also your content engine for the next workshop series.


---

### Cross-cutting design principles for all four workshops

**For 100 online participants:**

1. **WhatsApp as the nerve centre** — Use a group for pre-engagement (drop the exercise brief 24 hours before), real-time sharing during, and follow-up prompts after. You did this for LBSNAA and it worked.
2. **Google Form → live AI analysis** — Participants submit a short answer at the start of each session. You analyse it live with Claude. They see their collective intelligence reflected back at them. This is your signature move from Goodbye MBA and Deloitte — it never fails.
3. **Shared Google Doc as the artefact** — Every exercise output goes into one doc. Participants see each other's work. AI does a meta-analysis at the end. The doc is the deliverable they take home.
4. **Competitive framing** — This is an IIM crowd. "Who can find the most hallucinations?" "Whose context block produces the best output?" "Who completes the analysis fastest?" Score it. Leaderboard visible in real time.
5. **One WhatsApp prompt drop per day for 5 days after** — Keep the learning alive. Each prompt is a 2-minute exercise. Example: "Take any email you received today. Run this prompt on it: [investigative journalist analysis]. Share the most surprising thing you found."
6. **The "show don't tell" rule** — Every concept should be demonstrated, not explained. If you can't demo it in under 3 minutes, cut it. From your LBSNAA approach: *"This is one of those weird sessions where I'm literally telling you, don't listen to me. Open your laptops, use WhatsApp."*
7. **The participant as the dataset** — In each workshop, participants generate data (form responses, context files, analysis outputs) that becomes the content of the session. They are both the audience and the subject. This is more engaging than any prepared slide deck.

---

The durable skill is not magic wording. It is knowing what information, examples, constraints, rubrics, history, data, and exclusions the AI needs. Cover these topics: Start with the IIM alumni Singapore fear: “I don’t know the tools; my juniors are faster.” Flip it: their advantage is domain judgment. Repurpose: “Before meeting a client, don’t ask AI to ‘research the company.’ Give it the transcript of the last call, your CRM notes, the website, and your goal. Ask for a meeting brief with risks, likely objections, and exact questions.” Second, **WhatsApp/email mining**. Reuse the IIMB 2001 WhatsApp group example from Vibe Analytics: one-word “Analyze” gives generic output; a richer prompt asking for recent catch-up, surprising patterns, non-obvious insights, and a Malcolm Gladwell-style story gives memorable output. This lands well with alumni because they all live in WhatsApp groups. Third, **AGENTS.md / personal context file**. From your AI advice: keep reusable instructions, preferences, examples, and post-mortems. For executives, rename it as “My AI Operating Manual.” It should include: what I do, my industry, my recurring decisions, writing style, trusted sources, don’t-do rules, risk thresholds, and common deliverables. Fourth, **context boundaries and privacy**. Teach: never dump sensitive data blindly. Send schema instead of data where possible; use enterprise/API accounts for confidential data; anonymize; provide least-privilege access; use dedicated folders and read-only connectors. This matters especially in Singapore finance, healthcare, education, and regional HQ roles. Exercise for 100 online participants: Run a **Context Upgrade Race**. Give everyone the same weak prompt: “Analyze this customer feedback and suggest strategy.” Provide a small synthetic dataset: 30 feedback notes for a Singapore-based premium healthcare, wealth, education, or logistics service. Round 1: participants paste the weak prompt and score the result on usefulness. Round 2: they add context: audience, business goal, constraints, examples, local market, output format, and verification rubric. Round 3: they ask AI to interview them for missing context, then rerun. Collect answers in a Google Form. Live-cluster the differences. Show that better context beats better prose. Takeaway artifact: a one-page **Context Brief Template**: “Here is the task. Here is the business situation. Here are the source materials. Here are definitions. Here are examples. Here is what good looks like. Here is what to avoid. Here is the output format. Here is how to verify.” ## Session 2: AI tools & workflows — “Stop chatting. Start operating.” Core message: The personal productivity phase is over. The next step is workflow design: capture → ask → generate → verify → reuse → automate. Cover these topics: Open with your own “use AI 50 times a day” advice. But position it as a reflex-building drill, not a productivity hack. Leaders need volume because they cannot discover use cases intellectually; they discover them through repeated discomfort: “I’m stuck,” “I’m annoyed,” “I need a draft,” “I need a second opinion,” “I don’t know what to ask.” Then teach the workflow ladder: Level 1: Ask AI for answers. Level 2: Ask AI to ask you questions. Level 3: Ask AI to produce artifacts. Level 4: Ask AI to run tools/code. Level 5: Ask AI to maintain a reusable workflow. Level 6: Ask AI to monitor, verify, and escalate. Use your local examples: **Vibe coding for non-coders**: The MBA audience does not need syntax. They need specs, tests, and judgment. Show how a business leader can build a small tool: a reimbursement checker, board-pack summarizer, contract-risk triage, LinkedIn prospect briefing generator, or KPI variance explainer. **Derived formats**: One source becomes a memo, deck, podcast, infographic, quiz, video script, FAQ, and stakeholder email. This is excellent for alumni who lead teams: “Every meeting transcript should become five assets.” **AI workspace**: Project folder, prompts.md, AGENTS.md, skills folder, test files, git checkpoints. For non-technical users, simplify this to: “One folder per workflow. Keep prompts, examples, outputs, failures, and review notes.” **Tool choice**: Do not make this a shopping list. Teach selection by job: ChatGPT for reasoning, analysis, and structured outputs. Claude-style agents for long coding/writing workflows. Gemini/NotebookLM-style tools for Google Workspace, long documents, video/audio, and fast source summarization. Perplexity-style tools for cited current research. Excel/Sheets + AI for business users. Coding agents when the output must be runnable or repeatable. Because current tools change quickly, emphasize benchmarking on their own tasks. Recent market movement around Codex, Claude Code, and agent-first development tools reinforces the point: the interface is moving from “assistant beside the work” to “agent doing the work while you supervise.” ([TechRadar][2]) Exercise for 100 online participants: Run an **AI Workflow Relay**. Everyone chooses one of four personas: CFO, CHRO, sales leader, operations leader. Give each group the same raw input: a messy meeting transcript plus a small spreadsheet. Their mission: create a board-ready one-page recommendation. Step 1: AI extracts decisions, risks, numbers, owners. Step 2: AI creates a draft memo. Step 3: AI creates a slide outline. Step 4: AI creates a follow-up email. Step 5: AI writes a checklist to repeat the workflow next time. Participants submit their final workflow prompt, not just the output. Live-show 3–4 workflows and ask: “Could someone else run this tomorrow?” Takeaway artifact: **Workflow Card**. Each card has: trigger, inputs, AI task, human review, verification, output, reuse frequency, owner, risk level. ## Session 3: Agentic analysis — “Let AI analyze, but make it prove” Core message: AI can now perform consultant/analyst-style work: generate hypotheses, write code, test data, create summaries, produce decks. But it must be forced into evidence-bearing workflows. This is likely the most distinctive session for you. Cover these topics: Start with your “Goodbye MBA, Hello AI” strategy example: a healthcare client wants to switch patients from injections to pills. Traditional consulting flow: generate hypotheses, test with data, synthesize recommendations. Your demo had the LLM generate hypotheses, write Python, test significance, and summarize validated recommendations in minutes. This is perfect for IIM alumni because it directly threatens and upgrades classic MBA work. Teach the **agentic analysis loop**: Question → Hypotheses → Data needed → Code/test plan → Execution → Evidence → Recommendation → Critique → Red-team → Decision. Make the rule explicit: “For numbers, AI writes and runs code. It does not reason in prose.” Then teach verification patterns: Grounding: show sources and citations. Confidence: ask for what it knows, does not know, and assumes. Cross-checking: run multiple models or agents. Golden set: test on known examples. Code execution: make outputs reproducible. Exception triage: green/yellow/red routing. Human-on-the-loop: review disagreements and high-stakes outputs. Audit trail: preserve prompt, data, code, result, and review. Use your verifiable agents material: Sales intelligence example: 65 signals → observable facts → inferences → known unknowns → actions. The key is not the final recommendation; it is the traceability from action back to evidence. Contract analysis example: checklist × contract matrix with citations. This is immediately relatable to finance, legal, procurement, insurance, and compliance leaders. Multi-model verification example: one model has roughly 14% error in a classification task; requiring agreement across models can reduce error sharply while routing disagreements to humans. Present this as managerial design, not technical wizardry. InsurLE / rules example: LLM converts policy language into executable rules; once checked, claim decisions become auditable. This is the clearest “LLMs hallucinate, code doesn’t” story. Recent security research also supports a stronger version of this: agentic systems need controlled boundaries around prompts, tools, data, and context. Guardrails alone are probabilistic; enterprise workflows need policy, authentication, and runtime enforcement. ([arXiv][3]) Exercise for 100 online participants: Run a **Mini Consulting Case: AI Analyst vs Human Partner**. Give participants a synthetic business dataset: say 500 rows of customer churn, admissions conversion, wealth customer profitability, or B2B sales pipeline. Provide a business question: “Where should the CEO focus next quarter?” Teams use AI to: Generate 5 hypotheses. Pick 3 testable ones. Write analysis code or spreadsheet formulas. Produce a recommendation. Produce a verification appendix: assumptions, checks, edge cases, and what would change the recommendation. Then run a “partner review” round: each team swaps outputs with another team and asks AI to find flaws. The winner is not the best-looking answer. It is the answer with the best evidence trail. Takeaway artifact: **Agentic Analysis Checklist**. “Did it state the question? Did it separate fact from inference? Did it run code? Did it show sources? Did it test alternatives? Did it identify known unknowns? Did it make review easy?” ## Session 4: AI strategy — “Build AI-native operating leverage” Core message: AI strategy is not “which model should we buy?” It is: which workflows, decisions, assets, and relationships become more valuable when intelligence is cheap? Cover these topics: Start with the IIM/MBA framing: classic management still matters, but the bottleneck has moved. Generation is cheap. Scarcity is now context, trust, proprietary data, workflow integration, verification, taste, distribution, and accountability. Use your “agents are the new software” frame: Old software: deterministic apps, fixed UI, humans adapt to software. New software: agents, natural-language intent, tool use, memory, audit trails, humans supervise. Therefore, leaders must design work for teams of humans plus agents. Teach a strategy stack: 1. Use-case portfolio: productivity, process redesign, product innovation, new business model. 2. Context assets: transcripts, SOPs, domain maps, examples, rubrics, labeled cases, prompt libraries. 3. Workflow layer: where AI enters, what tools it can use, what it cannot do. 4. Verification layer: testing, human review, audit, escalation. 5. Adoption layer: power users, rituals, metrics, incentives. 6. Business model layer: outcome pricing, faster delivery, thinner teams, new services. Use Singapore-specific framing: Singapore alumni are likely in regional leadership, financial services, consulting, tech, education, healthcare, logistics, or family/business networks. Many will face security and governance blockers. So teach “safe acceleration”: use enterprise accounts, scoped data, synthetic data, red/yellow/green workflows, and audit trails. Singapore’s AI governance ecosystem is a feature, not a brake: it lets leaders move faster with trust. Use your local examples: **Reworld / enterprise pivot**: start where business pain and available data meet. Do not chase a generic AI platform. Use AI to find data quality fixes, stakeholder insights, revenue opportunities, and operational exceptions. **Innovation team demos**: 2–4 prototypes a day from interns/non-coders. Repurpose this for alumni: create a “demo factory” inside every function. The strategic question becomes: “What can we prototype by Friday that changes imagination?” **AI adoption advice**: leaders must visibly use AI. Adoption is not training completion. Measure unique days of use, task diversity, number of workflows created, prompts reused, outputs verified, and business decisions improved. **Outcome-based selling**: as software artifacts commoditize, sell accountability, trust, and verified outcomes. This is especially relevant for consultants, founders, investors, and functional leaders. Mention a current strategic tension: large AI vendors are moving up the stack into consulting, coding, workflows, and enterprise agents. This creates opportunity and threat for professional-services leaders; generic AI platforms will commoditize undifferentiated advisory, while trusted domain operators with proprietary context and verification can move up-market. ([Financial Times][4]) Exercise for 100 online participants: Run an **AI Strategy Tournament**. Each participant submits one workflow from their organization using this template: “Today, [role] spends [time] doing [task] using [inputs] to produce [output]. Errors/delays happen because [reason]. With AI, we can [new workflow]. Human review remains for [cases]. Value is [time/revenue/risk/customer impact].” AI clusters the 100 workflows live into themes: revenue, cost, risk, CX, employee productivity, product innovation. Break participants into groups. Each group picks one workflow and builds a mini strategy: What data/context is needed? What agent/tool does the work? What must be verified? What is the human role? What metric proves success in 30 days? What could go wrong? What is the 1-week prototype? Final vote: not “coolest AI idea,” but “most deployable in 30 days.” Takeaway artifact: **AI Strategy One-Pager**. Use case, business owner, user, current workflow, AI-native workflow, data/context required, risk class, verification design, adoption ritual, success metric, 30-day pilot. ## Suggested 90-minute structure for each session Use the same rhythm each time: First 10 minutes: sharp provocation and one personal story. Next 15 minutes: teach the framework. Next 15 minutes: live demo from your own work. Next 30 minutes: participant exercise. Next 10 minutes: live review of submissions. Final 10 minutes: “Monday morning playbook.” For 100 online participants, the most important design choice is to make them produce artifacts, not chat comments. ## Four 90-Minute Workshops for IIM Alumni Singapore

### Revised & Synthesised

#### The throughline (say this at the start of session 1)

> Session 1 teaches you to package your expertise as context. Session 2 teaches you to convert repeated work into reusable workflows. Session 3 teaches you to delegate analysis while keeping the evidence trail. Session 4 teaches you to turn all of that into operating advantage.
>
> The repeated mantra: **Use AI personally. Capture context systematically. Build workflows, not chats. Verify everything important. Measure adoption. Sell outcomes, not tools.**

#### Audience framing (applies to every session)

These are domain-rich, time-poor, governance-conscious seniors. Many have been exposed to Copilot or basic ChatGPT. Avoid "intro to GenAI." Every session answers one question: **what should a business leader personally do differently on Monday?**

The sharpest audience insight from your December 2025 IIM Alumni SG session: they fear irrelevance but undervalue what they actually have. The flip is the opening of every session — **their competitive advantage is not deep tech but deep domain. AI is the shovel; their domain knowledge is the map. Maps trump shovels.**


---

#### The 90-minute rhythm (use this structure in all four sessions)

| Minutes | Move |
| --- | --- |
| 0–10 | Sharp provocation + one personal story |
| 10–25 | Teach the framework |
| 25–40 | Live demo from your own work |
| 40–70 | Participant exercise |
| 70–80 | Live review of submissions |
| 80–90 | Monday morning playbook |

The design rule: **participants produce artifacts, not chat comments.** Google Forms/Sheets is the shared substrate. Short structured submissions. AI clusters and critiques their own answers live. They go from passive audience to dataset, case material, and co-authors.


---

### Session 1: Context Engineering

#### *"Your domain expertise is the moat — if you package it right"*

**Core message:** Prompt engineering is declining; context engineering is rising. The durable skill is not magic wording — it is knowing what information, examples, constraints, and history an AI needs to act on your behalf at the level you'd expect from a trusted senior.


---

**0–10: The provocation**

Open with this from your Goodbye MBA talk: *"The prompt engineering techniques that were actually true a year ago are no longer true. In some cases they make LLMs perform marginally worse."* The room has been told to "be specific" and "use system prompts." Show that newer models have absorbed those heuristics and no longer respond to them. Then: Gartner, July 2025 — "context engineering is in, prompt engineering is out." What replaced it?

**10–25: The framework — the context stack**

Context = **Goal + Role + Audience + Source material + Definitions + Examples + Constraints + Rubric + Output format + Verification method.**

Not all ten are needed every time. But knowing the stack tells you exactly what's missing when output is mediocre. The most commonly omitted: *examples of what good looks like* and *what to explicitly avoid*.

Show one prompt run without context → generic output. Same prompt with a five-sentence context block → night-and-day. This is the entire framework in two minutes.

**25–40: Demo — three examples from your own work**

*1. WhatsApp/email mining with context.* From December 2025 IIM session: "Analyze" → generic. Then the full investigative journalist prompt → "Wait, really?" insights. The richer prompt specifies audience (you, a returning alum), goal (catch up on what you missed), constraint (no boring stuff), and output format (narrative). This lands because they all live in WhatsApp alumni groups.

*2. Meeting prep from digital exhaust.* From your ai-advice.md: before a client meeting, don't ask AI to "research the company." Give it the transcript of the last call, your CRM notes, the website, and your goal. Ask for a brief with likely objections and exact opening questions. Demo this live. This is context engineering in professional practice.

*3. AGENTS.md / personal context file.* Introduce it as "My AI Operating Manual" for the executive audience. Contents: who I am, my industry, my recurring decisions, my writing style, my trusted sources, my don't-do rules, my risk threshold, my common deliverables. From your ai-advice.md: *"After each session, ask: 'What should we add to AGENTS.md based on what we just learned?' The agent updates its own instructions."* Demo: show yours. It should be surprising in its specificity.

**40–70: Exercise — Context Upgrade Race**

Give everyone the same weak prompt: *"Analyze this customer feedback and suggest strategy."* Provide a small synthetic dataset — 30 feedback notes for a Singapore-based premium service (wealth management, healthcare, or education — pick what fits the room).

* **Round 1:** Run the weak prompt. Score output on usefulness 1–5 via form.
* **Round 2:** Add context — business goal, audience, local market constraints, output format, verification rubric. Run again. Score.
* **Round 3:** Ask AI to *interview you* for missing context, then rerun.

Collect all three scores via Google Form. Show live: better context beats better prose, every time. The meta-lesson: their 20 years of domain knowledge is context waiting to be packaged.

**70–80: Live review**

Show three participant context blocks side by side. Ask the group: which one would you trust to brief a new analyst? What's missing from each?

**80–90: Monday morning playbook**

**Takeaway artifact — Context Brief Template (one page):**

> Here is the task. Here is the business situation. Here are the source materials. Here are definitions and terms I use specifically. Here is an example of what excellent output looks like. Here is what to avoid. Here is the output format. Here is how I will verify the result.

**One action:** Before the next session, build your own "AI Operating Manual" — 200 words, in this order: who you are, your industry, your top three recurring tasks, what good looks like, what AI should never assume about you.

**Privacy sidebar (essential for Singapore finance/legal/healthcare audience):** Never dump sensitive data blindly. Send schema, not data, where possible. Use enterprise or API accounts for confidential material. Grant read-only, scoped access. This is the one slide Singapore compliance-aware professionals will photograph.


---

### Session 2: AI Tools & Workflows

#### *"Stop chatting. Start operating."*

**Core message:** The personal productivity phase is over. The next phase is workflow design — turning repeated AI interactions into reusable, delegatable systems.


---

**0–10: The provocation**

From your ai-advice.md: *"Use AI 50 times a day."* Poll the room: how many times did you use AI yesterday? Typical answer: two to five. Then: how many times did you feel stuck, curious, annoyed, or uncertain? That gap is the opportunity. Volume is not vanity — it builds the reflex of reaching for AI *first*, which is what separates people who find AI useful from those who don't.

**10–25: The framework — the workflow ladder**

| Level | You do | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| 1 | Ask AI for answers | "What does this clause mean?" |
| 2 | Ask AI to ask *you* questions | "Interview me to clarify what I want" |
| 3 | Ask AI to produce artifacts | Draft, deck, code, brief |
| 4 | Ask AI to run tools/code | Analyse data, test hypotheses |
| 5 | Ask AI to maintain a reusable workflow | Prompt library, AGENTS.md |
| 6 | Ask AI to monitor, verify, escalate | Triage, exception flagging |

Most people in the room are at Level 1–2. This session gets them to Level 3–5. Level 6 is Session 3.

The tool selection principle: don't teach a shopping list. Teach selection by job.

| Job | Tool |
| --- | --- |
| Reasoning, structured analysis | ChatGPT extended thinking |
| Writing, coding, long workflows | Claude Sonnet |
| Google Workspace, audio, long docs | Gemini / NotebookLM |
| Cited current research | Perplexity |
| Running and repeating code | Claude Code / Codex |

From your SG Analytics session: *"Use Claude Sonnet for daily work. Reserve extended thinking for high-complexity bursts."* And the principle for the room: *"Get paid accounts for all three — $60/month. It's the highest ROI investment you'll make."* The model rankings shift monthly; what matters is benchmarking on your own specific tasks.

**25–40: Demo — from one source to five assets**

Take a 10-minute meeting transcript (use a synthetic one, or one from your own calls, anonymised). Show how it becomes: a decision log, an action email, a stakeholder briefing, a slide outline, a workflow prompt for next time. From your ai-advice.md: *"A single source can auto-generate a podcast, sketch note, executive summary, interactive explainer, quiz, slide deck, or narrative."*

Then show the "derived formats" sequence:

1. Transcript → AI extracts decisions, risks, numbers, owners
2. AI creates a draft memo
3. AI creates a slide outline
4. AI creates a follow-up email
5. AI writes a checklist to repeat this workflow next time

The key: Step 5 is what most people skip. That's the difference between a one-time interaction and a reusable workflow.

**40–70: Exercise — AI Workflow Relay**

Assign four personas (CFO, CHRO, Sales Lead, Ops Lead — roughly matching the room's composition). All groups get the same input: a messy meeting transcript plus a small spreadsheet with numbers.

Mission: produce a board-ready one-page recommendation using only AI.

Groups follow the five-step sequence above. **Participants submit their final workflow prompt, not just the output.** The question when reviewing: *"Could someone else on your team run this tomorrow without explanation?"*

Review three submissions live. That's the standard: a good workflow is one you never have to explain again.

**70–80: Live review**

Rank workflows on reusability. Show how minor additions (clearer inputs, explicit verification step, saved as a template) turn a one-off into infrastructure.

**80–90: Monday morning playbook**

**Takeaway artifact — Workflow Card:**

| Field | Your answer |
| --- | --- |
| Trigger | When does this workflow start? |
| Inputs | What goes in? |
| AI task | What does AI do? |
| Human review | What do *you* check? |
| Output | What comes out? |
| Reuse frequency | How often? |
| Risk level | Low / medium / high stakes |

**One action:** Pick your single most time-consuming recurring task. Before the next session, build one Workflow Card for it.


---

### Session 3: Agentic Analysis

#### *"Let AI analyse — but make it prove every step"*

**Core message:** AI can now perform consultant/analyst-level work — generate hypotheses, write and run code, test data, produce verified recommendations. But it must be forced into evidence-bearing workflows. Your job shifts from doing the analysis to directing it and holding it accountable.


---

**0–10: The provocation**

From your Goodbye MBA talk: a healthcare client wanted to know which patients to target for switching from injections to pills. Traditional consulting: hypotheses → data collection → statistical testing → synthesis → recommendation → 5 weeks, $50–100K. Your demo: LLM generates hypotheses, writes Python, runs significance tests, synthesises validated findings — in under five minutes. *"My former job as a BCG consultant is gone. Definitely by the time you get out of it. Of course the job will remain. What I was doing is gone."*

Then the update: since that demo, agents can now work for 12 continuous hours without intervention. From your Verifiable Autonomy talk: *"Tasks we aren't thinking of yet will start becoming far more doable."* From SG Analytics: research timelines of four to six weeks compressed to four to six hours. The bottleneck is no longer compute — it's the quality of the brief you give.

**10–25: The framework — the agentic analysis loop**

**Question → Hypotheses → Data needed → Code / test plan → Execution → Evidence → Recommendation → Critique → Red-team → Decision**

Two rules to make this enterprise-grade:

*Rule 1: For numbers, AI writes and runs code. It does not reason in prose.* From your ai-advice.md: *"LLMs hallucinate, but code doesn't. For math, logic, analysis: tell it to write and execute deterministic code. Code is binary — it works or fails."*

*Rule 2: Force it to separate facts from inferences from known unknowns.* From your Verifiable Autonomy demo (PGIM sales intelligence): 65 signals → observable facts with citations → inferences with confidence levels → known unknowns explicitly named. The final recommendation is only as trustworthy as this trail.

The verification toolkit:

* **Grounding:** sources and citations on every claim
* **Confidence:** what it knows, doesn't know, assumes
* **Cross-checking:** run same question through a second model — disagreement is your signal to verify manually
* **Code execution:** for anything numerical, make it reproducible
* **Exception triage:** green (auto-approve) / yellow (spot-check) / red (human required)
* **Audit trail:** prompt + data + code + result + review note — preserved

From your Verifiable Autonomy talk: *"74% of deployed enterprise agents still rely on manual verification. If you assume agentic AI across all use cases, it is untenable to rely on humans at every step."* The verification layer is how you close that gap without abandoning oversight.

**25–40: Demo — live agentic analysis**

Take a real Singapore business question from the room. Good options:

* "Who are the top five PE funds active in SEA infrastructure this year, and what signals suggest their next move?"
* "What is the current competitive landscape in [participant's industry]?"
* "What does the AI readiness of [a known regional bank] look like?"

Run it through Claude with web search. Show the chain: search → extract → synthesise → cite → flag uncertainty. Then deliberately find one claim that seems off. Cross-validate with ChatGPT. They disagree. *That disagreement is information.* This is the management insight dressed as a live demo.

**40–70: Exercise — Mini Consulting Case with Partner Review**

Give all participants a synthetic dataset (500-row customer churn, wealth client profitability, or B2B pipeline — pick one that fits the room). Business question: *"Where should the CEO focus next quarter?"*

Teams use AI to:

1. Generate five hypotheses
2. Pick three testable ones
3. Write analysis code or formulas
4. Produce a recommendation
5. Produce a **verification appendix**: assumptions made, checks run, edge cases, what would change the recommendation

Then: **partner review round.** Teams swap outputs. Each team asks AI to find flaws in the other team's analysis. Winner is not the best-looking answer — it is the answer with the most honest, complete evidence trail.

**70–80: Live review**

Show one strong and one weak verification appendix. The contrast makes the standard concrete.

**80–90: Monday morning playbook**

**Takeaway artifact — Agentic Analysis Checklist:**

> Did it state the question precisely? Did it separate facts from inferences? Did it run code for anything numerical? Did it show sources for every major claim? Did it test alternatives, not just the leading hypothesis? Did it name what it doesn't know? Did it make human review easy?

**One action:** Take one analysis your team produced last month. Run it through this checklist. How many boxes does it tick? How many would it tick if an agent had produced it with proper verification?


---

### Session 4: AI Strategy

#### *"Build AI-native operating leverage — not AI slideware"*

**Core message:** AI strategy is not "which model should we buy?" It is: which workflows, decisions, assets, and relationships become *more* valuable when intelligence is cheap? And which become irrelevant?


---

**0–10: The provocation**

*"Name an asset your organisation has built over the last 10 years that AI doesn't erode."* Take three answers from the room. Work through each honestly.

Then introduce the asset inversion table:

| Asset | With AI |
| --- | --- |
| Junior analyst hours | Depreciates — commoditised by agents |
| Process documentation | Depreciates — anyone can replicate |
| Domain expertise (as packaged context) | Appreciates — feeds better agents |
| Proprietary data and call transcripts | Appreciates — unique intelligence layer |
| Relationship trust and accountability | Appreciates — hardest to fake |
| Brand reputation for accuracy | Appreciates — scarcest in hallucination era |

From your Goodbye MBA: *"Sustainable differentiation lies not in the artifact itself, but accountability for the outcome and the trusted relationship that allows you to navigate the nuance."* From your Deloitte session: *"Software is a depreciating asset anyone can regenerate. Durable value: judgment, domain expertise, trust, and taking responsibility for results."* This is the IIM-compatible thesis — it maps onto frameworks they already own.

**10–25: The framework — a five-layer strategy stack**

1. **Use-case portfolio:** productivity (individual), process redesign (team), product innovation (organisation), new business model (market)
2. **Context assets:** transcripts, SOPs, domain maps, labeled examples, prompt libraries, rubrics — the institutional knowledge that becomes your AI's competitive edge
3. **Workflow layer:** where AI enters the process, what tools it can use, what it cannot do, what triggers human review
4. **Verification layer:** testing, audit trails, exception triage, escalation paths — this is what makes Horizon 2 deployable, not just demonstrable
5. **Adoption layer:** power users, visible leadership use, metrics (unique days of use, task diversity, workflows created — not training completion rates)

Then the business model implication: from your SG Analytics session, *"Research teams can transition to outcome-based pricing — clients pay per report based on satisfaction rather than fixed FTE costs."* When AI does the production, you charge for judgment, accountability, and verified outcomes. This is the up-market move for every professional services leader in the room.

**25–40: Demo — the "demo factory" concept**

From your innovation team: two to four working prototypes a day from interns and non-coders. The strategic question this unlocks: *"What can we prototype by Friday that changes what's imaginable?"*

Show one prototype built in under an hour: a board-pack summariser, contract-risk triage tool, LinkedIn prospect briefing generator, or KPI variance explainer. Non-coders, no syntax required — specs, tests, and judgment are the inputs. The MBA skill set is exactly right for this.

From your ai-advice.md: *"Build, don't plan. When execution is fast and cheap, don't agonise over the right solution. Build them all. Throw away what doesn't work."* And: *"Adding is easier than changing. Using AI to improve existing work has high inertia. Creating a new workflow has less competition."*

**40–70: Exercise — AI Strategy Tournament**

Each participant submits one workflow from their organisation using this template:

> "Today, [role] spends [X hours/week] doing [task] using [inputs] to produce [output]. Errors or delays happen because [reason]. With AI, the new workflow is [description]. Human review remains for [specific cases]. Value is [time saved / revenue / risk reduced / customer impact]."

AI clusters all 100 submissions live into themes: revenue, cost, risk, CX, employee productivity, product innovation. Show the distribution. This is immediate market research on AI priorities for Singapore business leaders — the room is its own case study.

Break into groups of five. Each group picks one workflow and builds a mini strategy using the five-layer stack: context needed, AI tool, verification design, human role, success metric in 30 days, one-week prototype.

**Vote — not on "coolest AI idea" but on "most deployable in 30 days."** The IIM crowd will take this seriously.

**70–80: Live review**

Debrief the winning workflow. What made it deployable? What's the one thing that would block it? (Usually: data access, governance approval, or the owner not being in the room.) That conversation is the strategy work.

**80–90: Monday morning playbook**

**Takeaway artifact — AI Strategy One-Pager:**

| Field |  |
| --- | --- |
| Use case |  |
| Business owner |  |
| Current workflow |  |
| AI-native workflow |  |
| Context / data required |  |
| Risk class (green/yellow/red) |  |
| Verification design |  |
| Adoption ritual |  |
| Success metric |  |
| 30-day pilot |  |

**The "impossibility list" — close all four sessions with this:** Ask each participant to submit one task they believe AI genuinely cannot do well yet. Collect all 100. Over the following month, systematically test them and share the results with the group. The list becomes a living community research project. Participants who submitted get the findings. It's also your content engine for the next series — and it keeps the IIM alumni network actively engaged long after the sessions end.

**Singapore-specific close:** Mention ECI (Enterprise Compute Initiative) co-funding as a risk-reduction mechanism for pilots. Frame Singapore's AI governance ecosystem (IMDA AI Verify, MAS guidance) not as a brake but as a feature — it lets you move faster with trust. Singapore enterprises are investing in agentic AI at 44%, far outpacing the global average of 34%. The room is already in the vanguard. The question is who builds the verification and context layer first. [Deloitte](https://www.deloitte.com/southeast-asia/en/about/press-room/agentic-and-physical-ai-set-for-rapid-growth-in-singapore-in-the-next-two-years.html)


---

### Structural design for 100 online participants (applies to all four sessions)

**Before each session:**

* Drop the exercise brief on WhatsApp 24 hours prior. One sentence: what to bring, what to download, what to think about.

**During each session:**

* Google Form for structured submissions (short, specific — not open text fields)
* Shared Google Sheet where outputs accumulate and are visible to all
* WhatsApp for real-time prompt sharing (paste the exact prompt participants should run)
* Live AI clustering of group responses — this is your signature move and it never fails

**After each session:**

* One prompt per day for five days on WhatsApp. Two minutes to run. Example from the context engineering session: *"Take any document you received this week. Add a five-sentence context block before asking AI to summarise it. Compare the output to what you'd have got without it. Post the before/after in one line."*

**The competitive layer (IIM alumni respond to this):** Every session has one competitive element. Context Olympics (Session 1), Task Race (Session 2), Partner Review (Session 3), Deployability Vote (Session 4). Score visible in real time. Prize: public recognition in the WhatsApp group and the post-session write-up.

**The artefact layer:** Four sessions, four named one-page takeaways — Context Brief Template, Workflow Card, Agentic Analysis Checklist, AI Strategy One-Pager. By the end of Workshop 4, every participant has a four-page operational toolkit they built themselves during the sessions.
